	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Victorian era</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Period of British history encompassing Queen Victoria's reign</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">

<p>In the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the United Kingdom">history of the United Kingdom</a>, the <b>Victorian era</b> was the period of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>'s reign, from 20 June 1837 until her death on 22 January 1901. The era followed the <a href="/wiki/Georgian_era" title="Georgian era">Georgian period</a> and preceded the <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian period</a>, and its later half overlaps with the first part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Belle_%C3%89poque" title="Belle Époque">Belle Époque</a></i> era of Continental Europe. In terms of moral sensibilities and political reforms, this period began with the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a>. There was a strong religious drive for higher moral standards led by the <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">nonconformist</a> churches, such as the <a href="/wiki/Methodists" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodists">Methodists</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Anglicanism" title="Evangelical Anglicanism">Evangelical wing</a> of the established Church of England. Britain's relations with the other <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)">Great Powers</a> were driven by the colonial antagonism of the <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">Great Game</a> with Russia, climaxing during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>; a <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Britannica" title="Pax Britannica">Pax Britannica</a></i> of international free trade was maintained by the country's naval and industrial supremacy. Britain embarked on global imperial expansion, particularly in Asia and Africa, which made the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires" title="List of largest empires">largest empire in history</a>. National self-confidence peaked.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ideologically, the Victorian era witnessed resistance to the <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> that defined the Georgian period and an increasing turn towards <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romanticism</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a> with regard to religion, social values, and arts.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Domestically, the political agenda was increasingly liberal, with a number of shifts in the direction of gradual <a href="/wiki/Reform_movement" title="Reform movement">political reform</a>, social reform, and the widening of the <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">franchise</a>. There were unprecedented demographic changes: the <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_England" title="Demography of England">population of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Wales" title="Demography of Wales">Wales</a> almost doubled from 16.8&#160;million in 1851 to 30.5&#160;million in 1901,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Scotland" title="Demography of Scotland">Scotland's population</a> also rose rapidly, from 2.8&#160;million in 1851 to 4.4&#160;million in 1901. However, <a href="/wiki/Irish_population_analysis" title="Irish population analysis">Ireland's population</a> decreased sharply, from 8.2&#160;million in 1841 to less than 4.5&#160;million in 1901, mostly due to emigration and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Famine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Between 1837 and 1901 about 15 million emigrated from Great Britain, mostly to the United States, Canada, South Africa, New Zealand, and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The two main political parties during the era remained the <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_faction)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political faction)">Whigs/Liberals</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a>; by its end, the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> had formed as a distinct political entity. These parties were led by such prominent statesmen as <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">Lord Melbourne</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith-Stanley,_14th_Earl_of_Derby" title="Edward Smith-Stanley, 14th Earl of Derby">Lord Derby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="William Gladstone">William Gladstone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>. The unsolved problems relating to <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_bills" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule bills">Irish Home Rule</a> played a great part in politics in the later Victorian era, particularly in view of Gladstone's determination to achieve a political settlement in Ireland.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology_and_periodisation">Terminology and periodisation</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Periodisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Periodisation">Periodisation</a></div>
<p>In the strictest sense, the Victorian era covers the duration of Victoria's reign as <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_monarchs" title="List of British monarchs">Queen of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>, from her accession on 20 June 1837—after the death of her uncle, <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a>—until her death on 22 January 1901, after which she was succeeded by her eldest son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">Edward VII</a>. Her reign lasted for 63 years and seven months, a longer period than any of her predecessors. The term 'Victorian' was in contemporaneous usage to describe the era.<sup id="cite_ref-plunkett_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plunkett-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The era has also been understood in a more extensive sense as a period that possessed sensibilities and characteristics distinct from the periods adjacent to it, in which case it is sometimes dated to begin before Victoria's accession—typically from the passage of or agitation for (during the 1830s) the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a>, which introduced a wide-ranging change to the <a href="/wiki/Voting_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Voting system">electoral system</a> of <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>. Definitions that purport a distinct sensibility or politics to the era have also created scepticism about the worth of the label "Victorian", though there have also been defences of it.<sup id="cite_ref-hewitt2006_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hewitt2006-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Sadleir" title="Michael Sadleir">Michael Sadleir</a> was insistent that "in truth the Victorian period is three periods, and not one".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> He distinguished early Victorianism – the socially and politically unsettled period from 1837 to 1850<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> – and late Victorianism (from 1880 onwards), with its new waves of <a href="/wiki/Aestheticism" title="Aestheticism">aestheticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> from the Victorian heyday: mid-Victorianism, 1851 to 1879. He saw the latter period as characterised by a distinctive mixture of prosperity, domestic <a href="/wiki/Prude" title="Prude">prudery</a>, and complacency<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> – what <a href="/wiki/G._M._Trevelyan" title="G. M. Trevelyan">G. M. Trevelyan</a> similarly called the "mid-Victorian decades of quiet politics and roaring prosperity".<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_and_diplomatic_history">Political and diplomatic history</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)">International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early">Early</span></h3>
<p>In 1832, after much <a href="/wiki/Days_of_May" title="Days of May">political agitation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act</a> was passed on the third attempt. The Act abolished many borough seats and created others in their place, as well as expanding the franchise in England and Wales (a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reform_Act_1832" title="Scottish Reform Act 1832">Scottish Reform Act</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_Reform_Act_1832" title="Irish Reform Act 1832">Irish Reform Act</a> were passed separately). Minor reforms followed in 1835 and 1836.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Destroying_Chinese_war_junks,_by_E._Duncan_(1843).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg/300px-Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg/450px-Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg/600px-Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1040" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Destroying_Chinese_war_junks,_by_E._Duncan_(1843).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> steamship <i><a href="/wiki/Nemesis_(1839)" title="Nemesis (1839)">Nemesis</a></i> (right background) destroying Chinese war <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junks</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Chuenpi" title="Second Battle of Chuenpi">Second Battle of Chuenpi</a> on 7 January 1841 during the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a> between Britain and the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>On 20 June 1837, Victoria became Queen of the United Kingdom on the death of her uncle, <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a>.  Her government was led by the <a href="/wiki/British_Whig_party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Whig party">Whig</a> prime minister <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">Lord Melbourne</a>, but within two years he had resigned, and the <a href="/wiki/Tories_(British_political_party)" title="Tories (British political party)">Tory</a> politician Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a> attempted to <a href="/wiki/Bedchamber_crisis" title="Bedchamber crisis">form a new ministry</a>. In the same year, a seizure of British opium exports to China prompted the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_India" title="Company rule in India">British imperial India</a> initiated the <a href="/wiki/First_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="First Anglo-Afghan War">First Anglo-Afghan War</a>—one of the first major conflicts of <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">the Great Game</a> between Britain and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-swisher248-50_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-swisher248-50-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1840, Queen Victoria married her German cousin <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg-Saalfield</a>. It proved a very happy marriage, whose children were much sought after by royal families across Europe. In 1840 the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi" title="Treaty of Waitangi">Treaty of Waitangi</a> established British sovereignty over New Zealand. The signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Treaty of Nanking</a> in 1842 ended the First Opium War and gave Britain control over <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Island" title="Hong Kong Island">Hong Kong Island</a>. However, a <a href="/wiki/1842_retreat_from_Kabul" title="1842 retreat from Kabul">disastrous retreat from Kabul</a> in the same year led to the annihilation of a British army column in Afghanistan. In 1845, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Famine</a> began to cause mass starvation, disease and death in Ireland, sparking large-scale emigration;<sup id="cite_ref-kinealyxv_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kinealyxv-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> To allow more cheap food into Ireland, the Peel government repealed the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a>. Peel was replaced by the Whig ministry of <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Lord John Russell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1853, Britain fought alongside <a href="/wiki/Second_French_Empire" title="Second French Empire">France</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> against Russia. The goal was to ensure that Russia could not benefit from the declining status of the Ottoman Empire,<sup id="cite_ref-taylor60-61_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor60-61-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> a strategic consideration known as the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Question" title="Eastern Question">Eastern Question</a>. The conflict marked a rare breach in the <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Britannica" title="Pax Britannica">Pax Britannica</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)">period of relative peace (1815–1914)</a> that existed among the <a href="/wiki/Great_Powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Powers">Great Powers</a> of the time, and especially in Britain's interaction with them. On its conclusion in 1856 with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1856)" title="Treaty of Paris (1856)">Treaty of Paris</a>, Russia was prohibited from hosting a military presence in the Crimea. In October of the same year, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium War</a> saw Britain overpower the Qing dynasty in China.
</p><p>During 1857–8, an <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">uprising</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sepoy" title="Sepoy">sepoys</a> against the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> was suppressed, an event that led to the end of Company rule in India and the transferral of administration to <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">direct rule</a> by the British government. The princely states were not affected and remained under British guidance.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle">Middle</span></h3>
<p>In 1861, Prince Albert died.<sup id="cite_ref-swisher248-50_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-swisher248-50-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In 1867, the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1867" title="Reform Act 1867">second Reform Act</a> was passed, expanding the franchise, and the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Act,_1867" title="Constitution Act, 1867">British North America Act</a> consolidated the country's possessions in that region into a <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Confederation" title="Canadian Confederation">Canadian Confederation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1878, Britain was a <a href="/wiki/Plenipotentiary" title="Plenipotentiary">plenipotentiary</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berlin_(1878)" title="Treaty of Berlin (1878)">Treaty of Berlin</a>, which gave <i>de jure</i> recognition to the independent states of <a href="/wiki/United_Principalities_of_Moldavia_and_Wallachia" title="United Principalities of Moldavia and Wallachia">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Serbia" title="Principality of Serbia">Serbia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Montenegro" title="Principality of Montenegro">Montenegro</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Evangelicals.2C_utilitarians.2C_and_reform"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Evangelicals,_utilitarians,_and_reform">Evangelicals, utilitarians, and reform</span></h3>
<p>The central feature of Victorian era politics is the search for reform and improvement, including both the individual personality and the society.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Three powerful forces were at work. First was the rapid rise of the middle class, in large part displacing the complete control long exercised by the aristocracy. Respectability was their code—a businessman had to be trusted, and must avoid reckless gambling and heavy drinking.  Second the spiritual reform closely linked to evangelical Christianity, including both the Nonconformist sects, such as the Methodists, and especially the evangelical or Low Church element in the established Church of England, typified by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley-Cooper,_7th_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley-Cooper, 7th Earl of Shaftesbury">Lord Shaftesbury</a> (1801–1885).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It imposed fresh moralistic values on society, such as Sabbath observance, responsibility, widespread charity, discipline in the home, and self-examination for the smallest faults and needs of improvement. Starting with the anti-slavery movement of the 1790s, the evangelical moralizers developed highly effective techniques of enhancing the moral sensibilities of all family members, and reaching the public at large through intense, very well organized agitation and propaganda. They focused on exciting a personal revulsion against social evils and personal misbehavior.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Asa_Briggs" title="Asa Briggs">Asa Briggs</a> points out, "There were as many treatises on 'domestic economy' in mid-Victorian England as on political economy"<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The third effect came from the philosophical <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism#Classical_utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarians</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a> (1748–1832), <a href="/wiki/James_Mill" title="James Mill">James Mill</a> (1773–1836) and his son <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> (1806–1873). They were not moralistic but scientific.  Their movement, often called "Philosophic Radicalism," fashioned a formula for promoting the goal of "progress" using scientific rationality, and businesslike efficiency, to identify, measure, and discover solutions to social problems. The formula was inquiry, legislation, execution, inspection, and report.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  In public affairs, their leading exponent was <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Chadwick" title="Edwin Chadwick">Edwin Chadwick</a> (1800–1890). Evangelicals and utilitarians shared a basic middle-class ethic of responsibility, and formed a political alliance. The result was an irresistible force for reform.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Social reforms focused on ending slavery, removing the slavery-like burdens on women and children, and reforming the police in order to prevent crime, rather than emphasizing the very harsh punishment of criminals.  Even more important were political reforms, especially the lifting of disabilities on nonconformists and Roman Catholics, and above all, the reform of Parliament and elections to introduce democracy and replace the old system whereby senior aristocrats controlled dozens of seats in parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>&lt;
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Punch_guy_fawkes_pope_1850.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Punch_guy_fawkes_pope_1850.jpg/220px-Punch_guy_fawkes_pope_1850.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Punch_guy_fawkes_pope_1850.jpg/330px-Punch_guy_fawkes_pope_1850.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Punch_guy_fawkes_pope_1850.jpg/440px-Punch_guy_fawkes_pope_1850.jpg 2x" data-file-width="930" data-file-height="1229" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Punch_guy_fawkes_pope_1850.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The restoration of the Catholic hierarchy in 1850 provoked a strong reaction.  This sketch is from an issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i>, printed in November that year.</div></div></div>
<p>Religion was a battleground during this era, with the Nonconformists fighting bitterly against the established status of the Church of England, especially regarding education and access to universities and public office. Penalties on Roman Catholics were mostly removed. The Vatican <a href="/wiki/Restoration_of_the_English_Catholic_hierarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Restoration of the English Catholic hierarchy">restored the English Catholic bishoprics</a> in 1850 and numbers grew through conversions and immigration from Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Secularism and doubts about the accuracy of the Old Testament grew as the scientific outlooked rapidly gained ground among the better educated. Walter E. Houghton argues, "Perhaps the most important development in 19th-century intellectual history was the extension of scientific assumptions and methods from the physical world to the whole life of man."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_Nonconformist_churches">Status of Nonconformist churches</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_conscience" title="Nonconformist conscience">Nonconformist conscience</a> describes the moral sensibility of the <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">Nonconformist</a> churches—those which dissent from the established <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>—that influenced British politics in the 19th and early 20th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In the 1851 census of church attendance, noncomformists who went to chapel comprised half the attendance of Sunday services.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Noncomformists were focused in the fast-growing urban middle class.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The two categories of this group were in addition to the evangelicals or "Low Church" element in the Church of England: "Old Dissenters," dating from the 16th and 17th centuries, included <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congregationalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregationalists">Congregationalists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterians</a> outside Scotland; "New Dissenters" emerged in the 18th century and were mainly Methodists. The "Nonconformist conscience" of the Old group emphasised religious freedom and equality, the pursuit of justice, and opposition to discrimination, compulsion, and coercion. The New Dissenters (and also the Anglican evangelicals) stressed personal morality issues, including sexuality, temperance, family values, and <a href="/wiki/Sabbath" title="Sabbath">Sabbath</a>-keeping. Both factions were politically active, but until the mid-19th century, the Old group supported mostly Whigs and Liberals in politics, while the New—like most Anglicans—generally supported Conservatives. In the late 19th century, the New Dissenters mostly switched to the Liberal Party. The result was a merging of the two groups, strengthening their great weight as a political pressure group. They joined together on new issues especially regarding schools and temperance, with the latter of special interest to Methodists.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> By 1914 the linkage was weakening and by the 1920s it was virtually dead.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Parliament had long imposed a series of political disabilities on Nonconformists outside Scotland. They could not hold most public offices, they had to pay local taxes to the Anglican church, be married by Anglican ministers, and be denied attendance at Oxford or degrees at Cambridge. Dissenters demanded the removal of political and civil disabilities that applied to them (especially those in the <a href="/wiki/Test_Acts" class="mw-redirect" title="Test Acts">Test</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corporation_Act_1661" title="Corporation Act 1661">Corporation Acts</a>). The Anglican establishment strongly resisted until 1828.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Dissenters organized into a political pressure group and succeeded in 1828 in repeal of some restrictions. It was a major achievement for an outside group, but the Dissenters were not finished and the early Victorian period saw them even more active and successful in eliminating their grievances.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Next on the agenda was the matter of church rates, which were local taxes at the parish level for the support of the parish church building in England and Wales. Only buildings of the established church received the tax money. Civil disobedience was attempted but was met with the seizure of personal property and even imprisonment. The compulsory factor was finally abolished in 1868 by <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>, and payment was made voluntary.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  While Gladstone was a moralistic evangelical inside the Church of England, he had strong support in the Nonconformist community.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Marriage_Act_1836" title="Marriage Act 1836">Marriage Act 1836</a> allowed local government registrars to handle marriages. Nonconformist ministers in their own chapels were allowed to marry couples if a registrar was present. Also in 1836, civil registration of births, deaths, and marriages was taken from the hands of local parish officials and given to local government registrars. Burial of the dead was a more troubling problem, for urban chapels had no graveyards, and Nonconformists sought to use the traditional graveyards controlled by the established church. The <a href="/wiki/Burial_Laws_Amendment_Act_1880" title="Burial Laws Amendment Act 1880">Burial Laws Amendment Act 1880</a> finally allowed that.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oxford University required students seeking admission to subscribe to the <a href="/wiki/39_Articles" class="mw-redirect" title="39 Articles">39 Articles</a> of the Church of England. Cambridge required that for a diploma. The two ancient universities opposed giving a charter to the new <a href="/wiki/University_of_London" title="University of London">University of London</a> in the 1830s because it had no such restriction. The university, nevertheless, was established in 1837, and by the 1850s Oxford dropped its restrictions. In 1871 Gladstone sponsored the <a href="/wiki/Universities_Tests_Act_1871" title="Universities Tests Act 1871">Universities Tests Act 1871</a> that provided full access to degrees and fellowships. Nonconformists (especially Unitarians and Presbyterians) played major roles in founding new universities in the late 19th century at <a href="/wiki/Victoria_University_of_Manchester" title="Victoria University of Manchester">Manchester</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/University_of_Birmingham" title="University of Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Liverpool" title="University of Liverpool">Liverpool</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leeds" title="University of Leeds">Leeds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agnostics_and_freethinkers">Agnostics and freethinkers</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ThomasHenryHuxley.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/ThomasHenryHuxley.jpg/220px-ThomasHenryHuxley.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/ThomasHenryHuxley.jpg/330px-ThomasHenryHuxley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/ThomasHenryHuxley.jpg/440px-ThomasHenryHuxley.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1167" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ThomasHenryHuxley.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">T. H. Huxley</a>'s <a href="/wiki/1860_Oxford_evolution_debate" title="1860 Oxford evolution debate">famous debate in 1860</a> with <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Wilberforce" title="Samuel Wilberforce">Samuel Wilberforce</a> was a key moment in the wider acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a></div></div></div>
<p>The abstract theological or philosophical doctrine of agnosticism, whereby it is theoretically impossible to prove whether or not God exists, suddenly became a popular issue around 1869, when <a href="/wiki/T._H._Huxley" class="mw-redirect" title="T. H. Huxley">T. H. Huxley</a> coined the term. It was much discussed for several decades, and had its own journal edited by <a href="/wiki/William_Stewart_Ross" title="William Stewart Ross">William Stewart Ross</a> (1844–1906) the <i>Agnostic Journal and Eclectic Review</i>. Interest petered out by the 1890s, and when Ross died the Journal soon closed. Ross championed agnosticism in opposition not so much to Christianity, but to atheism, as expounded by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bradlaugh" title="Charles Bradlaugh">Charles Bradlaugh</a><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The term "atheism" never became popular. <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">Blasphemy</a> laws meant that promoting atheism could be a crime and was vigorously prosecuted.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Southwell" title="Charles Southwell">Charles Southwell</a> was among the editors of an explicitly atheistic periodical, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Oracle_of_Reason" title="The Oracle of Reason">Oracle of Reason, or Philosophy Vindicated</a></i>, who were imprisoned for blasphemy in the 1840s.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Disbelievers call themselves "freethinkers" or "secularists". They included <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> They were not necessarily hostile to Christianity, as Huxley repeatedly emphasized. The literary figures were caught in something of a trap – their business was writing and their theology said there was nothing for certain to write. They instead concentrated on the argument that it was not necessary to believe in God in order to behave in moral fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The scientists, on the other hand, paid less attention to theology and more attention to the exciting issues raised by Charles Darwin in terms of evolution. The proof of God's existence that said he had to exist in order to have a marvelously complex world was no longer satisfactory when biology demonstrated that complexity could arise through evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_and_gender_roles">Family and gender roles</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Separate_spheres" title="Separate spheres">Separate spheres</a> and <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Victorian_era" title="Women in the Victorian era">Women in the Victorian era</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott_Omnibus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Scott_Omnibus.jpg/400px-Scott_Omnibus.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Scott_Omnibus.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="585" data-file-height="350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scott_Omnibus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_William_Joy" class="mw-redirect" title="George William Joy">George William Joy</a>'s painting <i>The Bayswater Omnibus</i>, 1895, depicts middle-class social life in this English Victorian-era street scene.</div></div></div>
<p>The centrality of the family was a dominant feature for all classes. Worriers repeatedly detected threats that had to be dealt with: working wives, overpaid youths, harsh factory conditions, bad housing, poor sanitation, excessive drinking, and religious decline. The licentiousness so characteristic of the upper class of the late 18th and early 19th century dissipated. The home became a refuge from the harsh world; middle-class wives sheltered their husbands from the tedium of domestic affairs. The number of children shrank, allowing much more attention to be paid to each child. Extended families were less common, as the nuclear family became both the ideal and the reality.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The emerging middle-class norm for women was <a href="/wiki/Separate_spheres" title="Separate spheres">separate spheres</a>, whereby women avoid the public sphere – the domain of politics, paid work, commerce and public speaking. Instead they should dominate in the realm of domestic life, focused on care of the family, the husband, the children, the household, religion, and moral behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Religiosity was in the female sphere, and the Nonconformist churches offered new roles that women eagerly entered. They taught in Sunday schools, visited the poor and sick, distributed tracts, engaged in fundraising, supported missionaries, led Methodist class meetings, prayed with other women, and a few were allowed to preach to mixed audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The long 1854 poem <i><a href="/wiki/The_Angel_in_the_House" title="The Angel in the House">The Angel in the House</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Patmore" title="Coventry Patmore">Coventry Patmore</a> (1823–1896) exemplified the idealized Victorian woman who is angelically pure and devoted to her family and home. The poem was not pure invention, but reflected the emerging legal economic social, cultural, religious and moral values of the Victorian middle-class. Legally women had limited rights to their own bodies, the family property, or their children. The recognized identities were those of daughter, wife, mother, and widow. Rapid growth and prosperity meant that fewer women had to find paid employment, and even when the husband owned a shop or small business, the wife's participation was less necessary. Meanwhile, the home sphere grew dramatically in size; women spent the money and decided on the furniture, clothing, food, schooling, and outward appearance the family would make. Patmore's model was widely copied – by Charles Dickens, for example.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Literary critics of the time suggested that superior feminine qualities of delicacy, sensitivity, sympathy, and sharp observation gave women novelists a superior insight into stories about home family and love. This made their work highly attractive to the middle-class women who bought the novels and the serialized versions that appeared in many magazines. However, a few early feminists called for aspirations beyond the home. By the end of the century, the "New Woman" was riding a bicycle, wearing bloomers, signing petitions, supporting worldwide mission activities, and talking about the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Feminists of the 20th century reacted in hostile fashion since the norm was still holding back their aspirations; <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> said the angel had to be killed.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_education_in_England#Nineteenth_century" title="History of education in England">History of education in England §&#160;Nineteenth century</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Public_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Public school (United Kingdom)">Public school (United Kingdom)</a></div>
<p>The era saw a reform and renaissance of public schools, inspired by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Arnold" title="Thomas Arnold">Thomas Arnold</a> at Rugby. The public school became a model for gentlemen and for public service.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_arts">Literature and arts</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Victorian_literature" title="Victorian literature">Victorian literature</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">prose</a>, the novel rose from a position of relative neglect during the 1830s to become the leading <a href="/wiki/Literary_genre" title="Literary genre">literary genre</a> by the end of the era.<sup id="cite_ref-plunkett_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plunkett-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-john2006_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-john2006-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In the 1830s and 1840s, the <a href="/wiki/Social_novel" title="Social novel">social novel</a> (also "Condition-of-England novels") responded to the social, political and economic upheaval associated with industrialisation. Though it remained influential throughout the period, there was a notable resurgence of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_fiction" title="Gothic fiction">Gothic fiction</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Fin_de_si%C3%A8cle" title="Fin de siècle">fin de siècle</a></i>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>'s novella <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Case_of_Dr_Jekyll_and_Mr_Hyde" title="Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde">Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde</a></i> (1886) and <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Picture_of_Dorian_Gray" title="The Picture of Dorian Gray">The Picture of Dorian Gray</a></i> (1891).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment">Entertainment</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fanque_at_Astley%27s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Fanque_at_Astley%27s.jpg/220px-Fanque_at_Astley%27s.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/08/Fanque_at_Astley%27s.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="227" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fanque_at_Astley%27s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pablo_Fanque" title="Pablo Fanque">Pablo Fanque</a> performing at <a href="/wiki/Astley%27s_Amphitheatre" title="Astley&#39;s Amphitheatre">Astley's Amphitheatre</a>, 1847</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Pollard_-_The_Derby_Pets-_The_Winner_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/James_Pollard_-_The_Derby_Pets-_The_Winner_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-James_Pollard_-_The_Derby_Pets-_The_Winner_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/James_Pollard_-_The_Derby_Pets-_The_Winner_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-James_Pollard_-_The_Derby_Pets-_The_Winner_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/James_Pollard_-_The_Derby_Pets-_The_Winner_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-James_Pollard_-_The_Derby_Pets-_The_Winner_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5105" data-file-height="3641" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Pollard_-_The_Derby_Pets-_The_Winner_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Epsom_Derby" title="Epsom Derby">Epsom Derby</a>; painting by <a href="/wiki/James_Pollard" title="James Pollard">James Pollard</a>, c. 1840</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Llandudno.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Llandudno.jpeg/220px-Llandudno.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Llandudno.jpeg/330px-Llandudno.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Llandudno.jpeg/440px-Llandudno.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="4676" data-file-height="2772" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Llandudno.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Llandudno" title="Llandudno">Llandudno</a>, 1856. With the arrival of the railway network, seaside towns became popular destinations for Victorian holiday makers</div></div></div>
<p>Popular forms of entertainment varied by social class. Victorian Britain, like the periods before it, was interested in literature (see <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Arthur Conan Doyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Bront%C3%AB" title="Charlotte Brontë">Charlotte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emily_Bront%C3%AB" title="Emily Brontë">Emily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Bront%C3%AB" title="Anne Brontë">Anne Brontë</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Makepeace_Thackeray" title="William Makepeace Thackeray">William Makepeace Thackeray</a>), theatre and <a href="/wiki/The_arts" title="The arts">the arts</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Aesthetic_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Aesthetic movement">Aesthetic movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-Raphaelite_Brotherhood" title="Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood">Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood</a>), and music, drama, and opera were widely attended. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Balfe" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael Balfe">Michael Balfe</a> was the most popular British <a href="/wiki/Grand_opera" title="Grand opera">grand opera</a> composer of the period, while the most popular musical theatre was a series of fourteen <a href="/wiki/Comic_operas" class="mw-redirect" title="Comic operas">comic operas</a> by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Gilbert and Sullivan">Gilbert and Sullivan</a>, although there was also <a href="/wiki/Victorian_burlesque" title="Victorian burlesque">musical burlesque</a> and the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_musical_comedy" title="Edwardian musical comedy">Edwardian musical comedy</a> in the 1890s. Drama ranged from <a href="/wiki/Low_comedy" title="Low comedy">low comedy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Henry_Irving" title="Henry Irving">Henry Irving</a>). There were, however, other forms of entertainment. Gentlemen went to dining clubs, like the <a href="/wiki/Beefsteak_club" class="mw-redirect" title="Beefsteak club">Beefsteak club</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Savage_club" class="mw-redirect" title="Savage club">Savage club</a>. Gambling at cards in establishments popularly called <a href="/wiki/Casino" title="Casino">casinos</a> was wildly popular during the period: so much so that evangelical and reform movements specifically targeted such establishments in their efforts to stop gambling, drinking, and prostitution.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brass_band_(British_style)" class="mw-redirect" title="Brass band (British style)">Brass bands</a> and 'The <a href="/wiki/Bandstand" title="Bandstand">Bandstand</a>' became popular in the Victorian era. The band stand was a simple construction that not only created an ornamental focal point, but also served acoustic requirements whilst providing shelter from the changeable <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Climate of the United Kingdom">British weather</a>. It was common to hear the sound of a brass band whilst strolling through <a href="/wiki/Urban_park" title="Urban park">parklands</a>. At this time musical recording was still very much a novelty.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Victorian era marked the golden age of the British circus.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Astley%27s_Amphitheatre" title="Astley&#39;s Amphitheatre">Astley's Amphitheatre</a> in Lambeth, London, featuring equestrian acts in a 42-foot wide circus ring, was the epicentre of the 19th century circus. The permanent structure sustained three fires but as an institution lasted a full century, with <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ducrow" title="Andrew Ducrow">Andrew Ducrow</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Batty" title="William Batty">William Batty</a> managing the theatre in the middle part of the century. <a href="/wiki/William_Batty" title="William Batty">William Batty</a> would also build his own 14,000-person arena, known commonly as Batty's Hippodrome, in Kensington Gardens, and draw crowds from the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_Exhibition" class="mw-redirect" title="Crystal Palace Exhibition">Crystal Palace Exhibition</a>. Travelling circuses, like <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Fanque" title="Pablo Fanque">Pablo Fanque</a>'s, dominated the British provinces, Scotland, and Ireland (Fanque would enjoy fame again in the 20th century when John Lennon would buy an 1843 poster advertising his circus and adapt the lyrics for <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> song, <i><a href="/wiki/Being_for_the_Benefit_of_Mr._Kite!" title="Being for the Benefit of Mr. Kite!">Being for the Benefit of Mr. Kite!</a></i>). Fanque also stands out as a black man who achieved great success and enjoyed great admiration among the British public only a few decades after Britain had abolished slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Fairsarefun.net_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairsarefun.net-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fairsarefun.net_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairsarefun.net-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another form of entertainment involved "spectacles" where paranormal events, such as <a href="/wiki/Mesmerism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesmerism">mesmerism</a>, communication with the dead (by way of <a href="/wiki/Mediumship" title="Mediumship">mediumship</a> or channelling), <a href="/wiki/Ghosts_in_European_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghosts in European culture">ghost</a> conjuring and the like, were carried out to the delight of crowds and participants. Such activities were more popular at this time than in other periods of recent Western history.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">Natural history</a> became increasingly an "amateur" activity. Particularly in Britain and the United States, this grew into specialist hobbies such as the <a href="/wiki/Ornithology" title="Ornithology">study of birds</a>, butterflies, seashells (<a href="/wiki/Malacology" title="Malacology">malacology</a>/<a href="/wiki/Conchology" title="Conchology">conchology</a>), beetles and wild flowers. Amateur <a href="/wiki/Collecting" title="Collecting">collectors</a> and natural history entrepreneurs played an important role in building the large natural history collections of the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Middle-class Victorians used the train services to visit the seaside, helped by the <a href="/wiki/Bank_Holiday_Act_1871" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank Holiday Act 1871">Bank Holiday Act of 1871</a>, which created a number of fixed holidays. Large numbers travelling to quiet fishing villages such as <a href="/wiki/Worthing" title="Worthing">Worthing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morecambe" title="Morecambe">Morecambe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_North_Yorkshire" title="Scarborough, North Yorkshire">Scarborough</a> began turning them into major tourist centres, and people like <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cook" title="Thomas Cook">Thomas Cook</a> saw tourism and even overseas travel as viable businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_the_United_Kingdom#History" title="Sport in the United Kingdom">Sport in the United Kingdom §&#160;History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Football_Match.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/A_Football_Match.jpg/220px-A_Football_Match.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/A_Football_Match.jpg/330px-A_Football_Match.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/A_Football_Match.jpg/440px-A_Football_Match.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2960" data-file-height="2212" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Football_Match.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rugby_football" title="Rugby football">Rugby football</a> match between England and Scotland, c. 1880</div></div></div>
<p>The Victorian era saw the introduction and development of many modern sports.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Often originating in the public schools, they exemplified new ideals of manliness.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">Cricket</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> cycling, <a href="/wiki/Croquet" title="Croquet">croquet</a>, horse-riding, and many water activities are examples of some of the popular sports in the Victorian era.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern game of tennis originated in Birmingham, England, between 1859 and 1865. The world's oldest tennis tournament, the <a href="/wiki/The_Championships,_Wimbledon" title="The Championships, Wimbledon">Wimbledon championships</a>, were first played in London in 1877. Britain was an active competitor in all the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> starting in 1896.
</p>
<h2><span id="Economy.2C_industry.2C_and_trade"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy,_industry,_and_trade">Economy, industry, and trade</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">Industrial revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Second industrial revolution">Second industrial revolution</a></div>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30em;&#32;; background-color: #ACE1AF;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">The most obvious and the most distinctive feature of the History of Civilisation, during the last fifty years [1837–87], is the wonderful increase of industrial production by the application of machinery, the improvement of old technical processes and the invention of new ones, accompanied by an even more remarkable development of old and new means of locomotion and intercommunication</div>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">—<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a><sup id="cite_ref-houghton5_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houghton5-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:From_%27Street_Life_in_London%27,_1877,_by_John_Thomson_and_Adolphe_Smith-_(6960859488).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/From_%27Street_Life_in_London%27%2C_1877%2C_by_John_Thomson_and_Adolphe_Smith-_%286960859488%29.jpg/230px-From_%27Street_Life_in_London%27%2C_1877%2C_by_John_Thomson_and_Adolphe_Smith-_%286960859488%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="295" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/From_%27Street_Life_in_London%27%2C_1877%2C_by_John_Thomson_and_Adolphe_Smith-_%286960859488%29.jpg/345px-From_%27Street_Life_in_London%27%2C_1877%2C_by_John_Thomson_and_Adolphe_Smith-_%286960859488%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/From_%27Street_Life_in_London%27%2C_1877%2C_by_John_Thomson_and_Adolphe_Smith-_%286960859488%29.jpg/460px-From_%27Street_Life_in_London%27%2C_1877%2C_by_John_Thomson_and_Adolphe_Smith-_%286960859488%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="2692" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:From_%27Street_Life_in_London%27,_1877,_by_John_Thomson_and_Adolphe_Smith-_(6960859488).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>''The accompanying photograph represents a second-hand clothes shop in a narrow thoroughfare of St. Giles, appropriately called Lumber Court, where several similar tradesmen are grouped together, all dealing in old clothes and furniture of a most varied and dilapidated description. ''</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Progress">Progress</span></h3>
<p>Historians have characterised the mid-Victorian era (1850–1870) as Britain's "Golden Years".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> There was prosperity, as the national income per person grew by half. Much of the prosperity was due to the increasing industrialisation, especially in textiles and machinery, as well as to the worldwide network of trade and engineering that produced profits for British merchants, and exports from<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> across the globe. There was peace abroad (apart from the short Crimean War, 1854–56), and social peace at home. Opposition to the new order melted away, says Porter. The <a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartist movement</a> peaked as a democratic movement among the working class in 1848; its leaders moved to other pursuits, such as trade unions and cooperative societies. The working class ignored foreign agitators like Karl Marx in their midst, and joined in celebrating the new prosperity. Employers typically were paternalistic and generally recognised the trade unions.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Companies provided their employees with welfare services ranging from housing, schools and churches, to libraries, baths, and gymnasia.  Middle-class reformers did their best to assist the working classes' aspirations to middle-class norms of "respectability".
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:279px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Sellers_of_Shell-fish_(6924988264).jpg" class="image"><img alt="from &#39;Street Life in London&#39;, 1877, by John Thomson and Adolphe Smith:" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_Sellers_of_Shell-fish_%286924988264%29.jpg/277px-The_Sellers_of_Shell-fish_%286924988264%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="277" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_Sellers_of_Shell-fish_%286924988264%29.jpg/416px-The_Sellers_of_Shell-fish_%286924988264%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/The_Sellers_of_Shell-fish_%286924988264%29.jpg/554px-The_Sellers_of_Shell-fish_%286924988264%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2680" data-file-height="2056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Sellers_of_Shell-fish_(6924988264).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A street stall in London during the 1870s</div></div></div>
<p>There was a spirit of libertarianism, says Porter, as people felt they were free. Taxes were very low, and government restrictions were minimal. There were still problem areas, such as occasional riots, especially those motivated by anti-Catholicism. Society was still ruled by the aristocracy and the gentry, who controlled high government offices, both houses of Parliament, the church, and the military. Becoming a rich businessman was not as prestigious as inheriting a title and owning a landed estate. Literature was doing well, but the fine arts languished as the Great Exhibition of 1851 showcased Britain's industrial prowess rather than its sculpture, painting or music. The educational system was mediocre; the main universities (outside Scotland) were likewise mediocre.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
Historian <a href="/wiki/Llewellyn_Woodward" title="Llewellyn Woodward">Llewellyn Woodward</a> has concluded:<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>For leisure or work, for getting or for spending, England was a better country in 1879 than in 1815. The scales were less weighted against the weak, against women and children, and against the poor. There was greater movement, and less of the fatalism of an earlier age. The public conscience was more instructed, and the content of liberty was being widened to include something more than freedom from political constraint ... Yet England in 1871 was by no means an earthly paradise. The housing and conditions of life of the working class in town &amp; country were still a disgrace to an age of plenty.</dd></dl>
<p>In December 1844 <a href="/wiki/Rochdale_Society_of_Equitable_Pioneers" title="Rochdale Society of Equitable Pioneers">Rochdale Society of Equitable Pioneers</a> founded what is considered the first <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperative</a> in the world. The founding members were a group of 28, around half of which were weavers, who decided to band together to open a store owned and managed democratically by the members, selling food items they could not otherwise afford. Ten years later, the British co-operative movement had grown to nearly 1,000 co-operatives. The movement also spread across the world, with the first <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_banking" title="Cooperative banking">cooperative financial institution</a> founded in 1850 in Germany.
</p>
<h2><span id="Technology.2C_science.2C_and_engineering"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology,_science,_and_engineering">Technology, science, and engineering</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stephenson%27s_Rocket.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Stephenson%27s_Rocket.jpg/220px-Stephenson%27s_Rocket.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Stephenson%27s_Rocket.jpg/330px-Stephenson%27s_Rocket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Stephenson%27s_Rocket.jpg/440px-Stephenson%27s_Rocket.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stephenson%27s_Rocket.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The railways changed communications and society dramatically</div></div></div>
<p>The Victorians were impressed by science and progress and felt that they could improve society in the same way as they were improving technology.  Britain was the leading world centre for advanced engineering and technology. Its engineering firms were in worldwide demand for designing and constructing railways.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A central development during the Victorian era was the improvement of communication.  The new railways all allowed goods, raw materials, and people to be moved about, rapidly facilitating trade and industry. The financing of railways became an important specialty of London's financiers.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The railway system led to a reorganisation of society more generally, with "railway time" being the standard by which clocks were set throughout Britain; the complex railway system setting the standard for technological advances and efficiency. Steam ships such as the <a href="/wiki/SS_Great_Britain" title="SS Great Britain">SS Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/SS_Great_Western" title="SS Great Western">SS Great Western</a> made international travel more common but also advanced trade, so that in Britain it was not just the luxury goods of earlier times that were imported into the country but essentials and raw materials such as corn and cotton from the United States and meat and wool from Australia. One more important innovation in communications was the <a href="/wiki/Penny_Black" title="Penny Black">Penny Black</a>, the first postage stamp, which standardised postage to a flat price regardless of distance sent.
</p><p>Even later communication methods such as electric power, <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a>, and telephones, had an impact. Photography was realised in 1839 by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Daguerre" title="Louis Daguerre">Louis Daguerre</a> in France and <a href="/wiki/William_Fox_Talbot" class="mw-redirect" title="William Fox Talbot">William Fox Talbot</a> in Britain. By 1889, hand-held cameras were available.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clifton.bridge.arp.750pix.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Suspension bridge between two brick-built towers, over a wooded gorge, showing mud and water at the bottom. In the distance are hills." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Clifton.bridge.arp.750pix.jpg/220px-Clifton.bridge.arp.750pix.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Clifton.bridge.arp.750pix.jpg/330px-Clifton.bridge.arp.750pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Clifton.bridge.arp.750pix.jpg/440px-Clifton.bridge.arp.750pix.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="545" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clifton.bridge.arp.750pix.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Brunel's <a href="/wiki/Clifton_Suspension_Bridge" title="Clifton Suspension Bridge">Clifton Suspension Bridge</a> in Bristol</div></div></div>
<p>Similar <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> reforms, prompted by the Public Health Acts <a href="/wiki/Public_Health_Act_1848" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Health Act 1848">1848</a> and 1869, were made in the crowded, dirty streets of the existing cities, and soap was the main product shown in the relatively new phenomenon of advertising. A great engineering feat in the Victorian Era was the <a href="/wiki/London_sewerage_system" title="London sewerage system">sewage system in London</a>. It was designed by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bazalgette" title="Joseph Bazalgette">Joseph Bazalgette</a> in 1858. He proposed to build 82&#160;mi (132&#160;km) of <a href="/wiki/Sewage_collection_and_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sewage collection and disposal">sewer system</a> linked with over 1,000&#160;mi (1,600&#160;km) of street <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_sewer" title="Sanitary sewer">sewers</a>. Many problems were encountered but the <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_sewer" title="Sanitary sewer">sewers</a> were completed. After this, Bazalgette designed the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Embankment" title="Thames Embankment">Thames Embankment</a> which housed sewers, <a href="/wiki/Water_pipe" class="mw-redirect" title="Water pipe">water pipes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a>. During the same period, London's <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a> network was expanded and improved, and a <a href="/wiki/Coal_gas" title="Coal gas">gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Cross-link" title="Cross-link">network</a> for <a href="/wiki/Gas_lighting" title="Gas lighting">lighting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gas_heater" title="Gas heater">heating</a> was introduced in the 1880s.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The model town of <a href="/wiki/Saltaire" title="Saltaire">Saltaire</a> was founded, along with others, as a planned environment with good sanitation and many civic, educational and recreational facilities, although it lacked a <a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pub</a>, which was regarded as a focus of dissent. During the Victorian era, science grew into the discipline it is today. In addition to the increasing professionalism of university science, many Victorian gentlemen devoted their time to the study of <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">natural history</a>. This study of natural history was most powerfully advanced by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> and his theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> first published in his book <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i> in 1859.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Close_No._101_High_Street_(-8)_LACMA_M.2008.40.98.8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Close_No._101_High_Street_%28-8%29_LACMA_M.2008.40.98.8.jpg/170px-Close_No._101_High_Street_%28-8%29_LACMA_M.2008.40.98.8.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Close_No._101_High_Street_%28-8%29_LACMA_M.2008.40.98.8.jpg/255px-Close_No._101_High_Street_%28-8%29_LACMA_M.2008.40.98.8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Close_No._101_High_Street_%28-8%29_LACMA_M.2008.40.98.8.jpg/340px-Close_No._101_High_Street_%28-8%29_LACMA_M.2008.40.98.8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1630" data-file-height="2100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Close_No._101_High_Street_(-8)_LACMA_M.2008.40.98.8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a> slum in 1871</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Railway_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_501975.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Railway_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_501975.jpg/220px-Railway_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_501975.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Railway_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_501975.jpg/330px-Railway_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_501975.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Railway_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_501975.jpg/440px-Railway_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_501975.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Railway_Bridge_-_geograph.org.uk_-_501975.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crossing the <a href="/wiki/Lagan_Canal" title="Lagan Canal">Lagan Canal</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Railway" title="Ulster Railway">Ulster Railway</a> near <a href="/wiki/Moira,_County_Down" title="Moira, County Down">Moira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, a legacy of the Victorian era.</div></div></div>
<p>Although initially developed in the early years of the 19th century, gas lighting became widespread during the Victorian era in industry, homes, public buildings and the <a href="/wiki/Street_light" title="Street light">streets</a>. The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">incandescent</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas_mantle" title="Gas mantle">gas mantle</a> in the 1890s greatly improved light output and ensured its survival as late as the 1960s. Hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Gasworks" title="Gasworks">gasworks</a> were constructed in cities and towns across the country. In 1882, incandescent <a href="/wiki/Electric_light" title="Electric light">electric lights</a> were introduced to London streets, although it took many years before they were installed everywhere.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Railways">Railways</span></h3>
<p>One of the great achievements of the Industrial Revolution in Britain was the introduction and advancement of railway systems, not only in the United Kingdom and the British Empire but across the world. British engineers and financiers designed, built and funded many major systems. They retained an ownership share even while turning over management to locals; that ownership was largely liquidated in 1914–1916 to pay for the World War. Railroads originated in England because industrialists had already discovered the need for inexpensive transportation to haul coal for the new steam engines, to supply parts to specialized factories, and to take products to market. The existing system of canals was inexpensive but was too slow and too limited in geography.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The engineers and businessmen needed to create and finance a railway system were available; they knew how to invent, to build, and to finance a large complex system. The first quarter of the 19th century involved numerous experiments with locomotives and rail technology. By 1825 railways were commercially feasible, as demonstrated by <a href="/wiki/George_Stephenson" title="George Stephenson">George Stephenson</a> (1791–1848) when he built the <a href="/wiki/Stockton_and_Darlington" class="mw-redirect" title="Stockton and Darlington">Stockton and Darlington</a>. On his first run, his locomotive pulled 38 freight and passenger cars at speeds as high as 12 miles per hour. Stephenson went on to design many more railways and is best known for standardizing designs, such as the "<a href="/wiki/Standard_gauge" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard gauge">standard gauge</a>" of rail spacing, at 4 feet 8½ inches.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Brassey" title="Thomas Brassey">Thomas Brassey</a> (1805–70) was even more prominent, operating construction crews that at one point in the 1840s totalled 75,000 men throughout Europe, the British Empire, and Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Brassey took thousands of British engineers and mechanics across the globe to build new lines. They invented and improved thousands of mechanical devices, and developed the science of civil engineering to build roadways, tunnels and bridges.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Britain had a superior financial system based in London that funded both the railways in Britain and also in many other parts of the world, including the United States, up until 1914. The boom years were 1836 and 1845–47 when Parliament authorised 8,000 miles of lines at a projected cost of £200 million, which was about the same value as the country's annual Gross Domestic Product (GDP) at that time.
