A new railway needed a charter, which typically cost over £200,000 (about $1 million) to obtain from Parliament, but opposition could effectively prevent its construction. The canal companies, unable or unwilling to upgrade their facilities to compete with railways, used political power to try to stop them. The railways responded by purchasing about a fourth of the canal system, in part to get the right of way, and in part to buy off critics. Once a charter was obtained, there was little government regulation, as laissez-faire and private ownership had become accepted practices.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The different lines typically had exclusive territory, but given the compact size of Britain, this meant that multiple competing lines could provide service between major cities. <a href="/wiki/George_Hudson" title="George Hudson">George Hudson</a> (1800–1871) became the "railway king" of Britain. He merged various independent lines and set up a "Clearing House" in 1842 which rationalized interconnections by establishing uniform paperwork and standard methods for transferring passengers and freight between lines, and rates when one system used freight cars owned by another. By 1850, rates had fallen to a penny a ton mile for coal, at speeds of up to fifty miles an hour. Britain now had had the model for the world in a well integrated, well-engineered system that allowed fast, cheap movement of freight and people, and which could be replicated in other major nations.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holl_(after_Frith)_The_Railway_Station_colorized.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Holl_%28after_Frith%29_The_Railway_Station_colorized.jpg/280px-Holl_%28after_Frith%29_The_Railway_Station_colorized.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Holl_%28after_Frith%29_The_Railway_Station_colorized.jpg/420px-Holl_%28after_Frith%29_The_Railway_Station_colorized.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Holl_%28after_Frith%29_The_Railway_Station_colorized.jpg/560px-Holl_%28after_Frith%29_The_Railway_Station_colorized.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3083" data-file-height="1444" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holl_(after_Frith)_The_Railway_Station_colorized.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Powell_Frith" title="William Powell Frith">Frith</a>'s depiction of <a href="/wiki/London_Paddington_station" title="London Paddington station">Paddington railway station</a> in London.</div></div></div>
<p>The railways directly or indirectly employed tens of thousands of engineers, mechanics, repairmen and technicians, as well as statisticians and financial planners. They developed new and more efficient and less expensive techniques. Most important, they created a mindset of how technology could be used in many different forms of business. Railways had a major impact on industrialization. By lowering transportation costs, they reduced costs for all industries moving supplies and finished goods, and they increased demand for the production of all the inputs needed for the railroad system itself. By 1880, there were 13,500 locomotives which each carried 97,800 passengers a year, or 31,500 tons of freight.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>India provides an example of the London-based financiers pouring money and expertise into a very well built system designed for military reasons (after the Mutiny of 1857), and with the hope that it would stimulate industry. The system was overbuilt and much too elaborate and expensive for the small amount of freight traffic it carried. However, it did capture the imagination of the Indians, who saw their railways as the symbol of an industrial modernity—but one that was not realized until a century or so later.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_medicine">Health and medicine</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clover_with_his_chloroform_apparatus_1862.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Clover_with_his_chloroform_apparatus_1862.jpg/170px-Clover_with_his_chloroform_apparatus_1862.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Clover_with_his_chloroform_apparatus_1862.jpg/255px-Clover_with_his_chloroform_apparatus_1862.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Clover_with_his_chloroform_apparatus_1862.jpg/340px-Clover_with_his_chloroform_apparatus_1862.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2265" data-file-height="3338" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clover_with_his_chloroform_apparatus_1862.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Thomas_Clover" title="Joseph Thomas Clover">Joseph Thomas Clover</a> demonstrating the Chloroform apparatus he invented in 1862</div></div></div>
<p>Medicine progressed during Queen Victoria's reign. Although <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>, or laughing gas, had been proposed as an <a href="/wiki/Anaesthetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaesthetic">anaesthetic</a> as far back as 1799 by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>, it wasn't until 1846 when an American dentist named <a href="/wiki/William_T.G._Morton" class="mw-redirect" title="William T.G. Morton">William Morton</a> started using <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">ether</a> on his patients that anaesthetics became common in the medical profession.<sup id="cite_ref-William_Morton_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-William_Morton-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In 1847 <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a> was introduced as an anaesthetic by <a href="/wiki/James_Young_Simpson" title="James Young Simpson">James Young Simpson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-chloroform_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chloroform-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Chloroform was favoured by doctors and hospital staff because it is much less flammable than ether, but critics complained that it could cause the patient to have a heart attack.<sup id="cite_ref-chloroform_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chloroform-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Chloroform gained in popularity in England and Germany after <a href="/wiki/John_Snow_(physician)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Snow (physician)">John Snow</a> gave Queen Victoria chloroform for the birth of her eighth child (Prince Leopold).<sup id="cite_ref-Chloroform_and_Queen_Victoria_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chloroform_and_Queen_Victoria-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> By 1920, chloroform was used in 80 to 95% of all narcoses performed in the UK and German-speaking countries.<sup id="cite_ref-chloroform_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chloroform-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Anaesthetics made painless <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a> possible. At the same time sugar consumption in the British diet increased, greatly increasing instances of <a href="/wiki/Tooth_decay" title="Tooth decay">tooth decay</a> .<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Dentures_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Dentures-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> As a result, more and more people were having teeth extracted and needing <a href="/wiki/Dentures" title="Dentures">dentures</a>. This gave rise to "Waterloo Teeth", which were real human teeth set into hand-carved pieces of <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamus</a> or <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a> jaws.<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Dentures_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Dentures-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Waterloo-Teeth_1_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waterloo-Teeth_1-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The teeth were obtained from executed criminals, victims of battlefields, from grave-robbers, and were even bought directly from the desperately impoverished.<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Dentures_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Dentures-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Medicine also benefited from the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Antiseptics" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiseptics">antiseptics</a> by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Joseph Lister</a> in 1867 in the form of carbolic acid (<a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Joseph_Lister_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joseph_Lister-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> He instructed the hospital staff to wear gloves and wash their hands, instruments, and dressings with a phenol solution and in 1869, he invented a machine that would spray carbolic acid in the operating theatre during surgery.<sup id="cite_ref-Joseph_Lister_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joseph_Lister-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Allen_%26_Sons_Cocoa_ad_1880.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5a/Allen_%26_Sons_Cocoa_ad_1880.jpg/330px-Allen_%26_Sons_Cocoa_ad_1880.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5a/Allen_%26_Sons_Cocoa_ad_1880.jpg/495px-Allen_%26_Sons_Cocoa_ad_1880.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5a/Allen_%26_Sons_Cocoa_ad_1880.jpg/660px-Allen_%26_Sons_Cocoa_ad_1880.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="891" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Allen_%26_Sons_Cocoa_ad_1880.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1880 London magazine ad links prosperity to temperance.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h2>
<p>The Victorian era was a time of unprecedented population growth in Britain. The population rose from 13.9&#160;million in 1831 to 32.5&#160;million in 1901. Two major contributary factors were fertility rates and mortality rates. Britain was the first country to undergo the <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">demographic transition</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">Agricultural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolutions</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Pollard_-_The_Louth-London_Royal_Mail_Travelling_by_Train_from_Peterborough_East,_Northamptonshire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/James_Pollard_-_The_Louth-London_Royal_Mail_Travelling_by_Train_from_Peterborough_East%2C_Northamptonshire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/230px-James_Pollard_-_The_Louth-London_Royal_Mail_Travelling_by_Train_from_Peterborough_East%2C_Northamptonshire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/James_Pollard_-_The_Louth-London_Royal_Mail_Travelling_by_Train_from_Peterborough_East%2C_Northamptonshire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/345px-James_Pollard_-_The_Louth-London_Royal_Mail_Travelling_by_Train_from_Peterborough_East%2C_Northamptonshire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/James_Pollard_-_The_Louth-London_Royal_Mail_Travelling_by_Train_from_Peterborough_East%2C_Northamptonshire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/460px-James_Pollard_-_The_Louth-London_Royal_Mail_Travelling_by_Train_from_Peterborough_East%2C_Northamptonshire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4921" data-file-height="3641" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Pollard_-_The_Louth-London_Royal_Mail_Travelling_by_Train_from_Peterborough_East,_Northamptonshire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Louth-London <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> travelling by train from <a href="/wiki/Peterborough_East_railway_station" title="Peterborough East railway station">Peterborough East</a>, 1845</div></div></div>
<p>Britain had the lead in rapid economic and population growth. At the time, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Robert_Malthus" title="Thomas Robert Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> believed this lack of growth outside Britain was due to the '<a href="/wiki/Malthusian_trap" title="Malthusian trap">Malthusian trap</a>'. That is, the tendency of a population to expand geometrically while resources grew more slowly, reaching a crisis (such as famine, war, or epidemic) which would reduce the population to a sustainable size. Britain escaped the 'Malthusian trap' because the Industrial Revolution had a positive impact on living standards.<sup id="cite_ref-EssayEL_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EssayEL-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> People had more money and could improve their standards; therefore, a population increase was sustainable.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertility_rates">Fertility rates</span></h3>
<p>In the Victorian era, <a href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertility rate">fertility rates</a> increased in every decade until 1901, when the rates started evening out.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> There were several reasons for this. One is biological: with improving living standards, a higher proportion of women were biologically able to have children. Another possible explanation is social. In the 19th century, the marriage rate increased, and people were getting married at a very young age until the end of the century, when the average age of marriage started to increase again slowly. The reasons why people got married younger and more frequently are uncertain. One theory is that greater prosperity allowed people to finance marriage and new households earlier than previously possible. With more births within marriage, it seems inevitable that marriage rates and birth rates would rise together.
</p><p>Birth rates were originally measured by the '<a href="/wiki/Crude_birth_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude birth rate">crude birth rate</a>'&#160;– births per year divided by total population. This is indeed a crude measure, as key groups and their fertility rates are not clear. It is likely to be affected mainly by changes in the age distribution of the population. The Net Reproduction Rate was then introduced as an alternative measure: it measures the average fertility rate of women of child-bearing ages.
</p><p>High rates of birth also occurred because of a lack of <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a>. Mainly because women lacked knowledge of birth control methods and the practice was seen as unrespectable.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The evening out of fertility rates at the beginning of the 20th century was mainly the result of a few big changes: availability of forms of birth control, and changes in people's attitude towards sex.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mortality_rates">Mortality rates</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rates" class="mw-redirect" title="Mortality rates">mortality rates</a> in England changed greatly through the 19th century. There was no catastrophic epidemic or famine in England or Scotland in the 19th century&#160;– it was the first century in which a major epidemic did not occur throughout the whole country, and deaths per 1000 of population per year in England and Wales fell from 21.9 from 1848 to 1854 to 17 in 1901 (cf, for instance, 5.4 in 1971).<sup id="cite_ref-victorian_mortality_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-victorian_mortality-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Social class had a significant effect on mortality rates: the upper classes had a lower rate of premature death early in the 19th century than poorer classes did.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Environmental and health standards rose throughout the Victorian era; improvements in nutrition may also have played a role, although the importance of this is debated.<sup id="cite_ref-victorian_mortality_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-victorian_mortality-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Sewage works were improved, as was the quality of drinking water. With a healthier environment, diseases were caught less easily and did not spread as much. Technology improved because the population had more money to spend on medical technology (for example, techniques to prevent death in childbirth, so that more women and children survived), which also led to a greater number of cures for diseases. However, there was a <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> epidemic in London in 1848–49, which killed 14,137 people, and another in 1853 killing 10,738. Reformers rushed to complete a modern <a href="/wiki/London_sewerage_system" title="London sewerage system">London sewerage system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">Tuberculosis</a> (spread in congested dwellings), lung diseases from the mines and <a href="/wiki/Typhoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a> remained common.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="High_culture">High culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Victorian_literature" title="Victorian literature">Victorian literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victorian_architecture" title="Victorian architecture">Victorian architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victorian_decorative_arts" title="Victorian decorative arts">Victorian decorative arts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Victorian_fashion" title="Victorian fashion">Victorian fashion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leadenhall_Street_J_Hopkins.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Leadenhall_Street_J_Hopkins.jpg/220px-Leadenhall_Street_J_Hopkins.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Leadenhall_Street_J_Hopkins.jpg/330px-Leadenhall_Street_J_Hopkins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Leadenhall_Street_J_Hopkins.jpg/440px-Leadenhall_Street_J_Hopkins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leadenhall_Street_J_Hopkins.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A picture of <a href="/wiki/Leadenhall_Street" title="Leadenhall Street">Leadenhall Street</a>, London, c. 1837</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louise_Rayner_Salisbury_The_Poultry_Cross.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Louise_Rayner_Salisbury_The_Poultry_Cross.jpg/230px-Louise_Rayner_Salisbury_The_Poultry_Cross.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Louise_Rayner_Salisbury_The_Poultry_Cross.jpg/345px-Louise_Rayner_Salisbury_The_Poultry_Cross.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Louise_Rayner_Salisbury_The_Poultry_Cross.jpg/460px-Louise_Rayner_Salisbury_The_Poultry_Cross.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2552" data-file-height="1738" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louise_Rayner_Salisbury_The_Poultry_Cross.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Poultry Cross, <a href="/wiki/Salisbury" title="Salisbury">Salisbury</a>, painted by <a href="/wiki/Louise_Rayner" title="Louise Rayner">Louise Rayner</a>, c. 1870</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic Revival architecture</a> became increasingly significant during the period, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Styles" title="Battle of the Styles">Battle of the Styles</a> between Gothic and <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">Classical</a> ideals. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Barry" title="Charles Barry">Charles Barry</a>'s architecture for the new <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a>, which had been badly damaged in an <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_Parliament" title="Burning of Parliament">1834 fire</a>, was built in the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_architecture" title="Medieval architecture">medieval style</a> of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Hall">Westminster Hall</a>, the surviving part of the building. It constructed a narrative of cultural continuity, set in opposition to the violent disjunctions of <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">Revolutionary France</a>, a comparison common to the period, as expressed in <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_French_Revolution:_A_History" title="The French Revolution: A History">The French Revolution: A History</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Expectations" title="Great Expectations">Great Expectations</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Tale_of_Two_Cities" title="A Tale of Two Cities">A Tale of Two Cities</a></i>. Gothic was also supported by critic <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>, who argued that it epitomised communal and inclusive social values, as opposed to Classicism, which he considered to epitomise mechanical standardisation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The middle of the 19th century saw <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Exhibition" class="mw-redirect" title="The Great Exhibition">The Great Exhibition</a> of 1851, the first <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Fair" class="mw-redirect" title="World&#39;s Fair">World's Fair</a>, which showcased the greatest innovations of the century. At its centre was <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">the Crystal Palace</a>, a modular glass and iron structure&#160;– the first of its kind. It was condemned by Ruskin as the very model of mechanical dehumanisation in design but later came to be presented as the prototype of <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">Modern architecture</a>. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_photography" title="History of photography">emergence of photography</a>, showcased at the Great Exhibition, resulted in significant changes in Victorian art with Queen Victoria being the first British monarch to be photographed. <a href="/wiki/John_Everett_Millais" title="John Everett Millais">John Everett Millais</a> was influenced by photography (notably in his portrait of Ruskin) as were other <a href="/wiki/Pre-Raphaelite_Brotherhood" title="Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood">Pre-Raphaelite</a> artists. It later became associated with the <a href="/wiki/Impressionism" title="Impressionism">Impressionistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_realism" title="Social realism">Social Realist</a> techniques that would dominate the later years of the period in the work of artists such as <a href="/wiki/Walter_Sickert" title="Walter Sickert">Walter Sickert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Holl" title="Frank Holl">Frank Holl</a>.
</p><p>The long-term effect of the reform movements was to tightly link the nonconformist element with the Liberal party. The dissenters gave significant support to moralistic issues, such as temperance and sabbath enforcement. The <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_conscience" title="Nonconformist conscience">nonconformist conscience</a>, as it was called, was repeatedly called upon by Gladstone for support for his moralistic foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In election after election, Protestant ministers rallied their congregations to the Liberal ticket. In Scotland, the Presbyterians played a similar role to the Nonconformist Methodists, Baptists and other groups in England and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The political strength of Dissent faded sharply after 1920 with the secularization of British society in the 20th century.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_middle_class">The middle class</span></h2>
<p>The rise of the middle class during the era had a formative effect on its character; the historian <a href="/wiki/Walter_Houghton" title="Walter Houghton">Walter E. Houghton</a> reflects that "once the middle class attained political as well as financial eminence, their social influence became decisive. The Victorian frame of mind is largely composed of their characteristic modes of thought and feeling".<sup id="cite_ref-houghton1_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houghton1-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Industrialisation brought with it a rapidly growing middle class whose increase in numbers had a significant effect on the social strata itself: cultural norms, lifestyle, values and morality. Identifiable characteristics came to define the middle-class home and lifestyle. Previously, in town and city, residential space was adjacent to or incorporated into the work site, virtually occupying the same geographical space. The difference between private life and commerce was a fluid one distinguished by an informal demarcation of function. In the Victorian era, English family life increasingly became compartmentalised, the home a self-contained structure housing a nuclear family extended according to need and circumstance to include blood relations. The concept of "privacy" became a hallmark of the middle-class life. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The English home closed up and darkened over the decade (1850s), the cult of domesticity matched by a cult of privacy. Bourgeois existence was a world of interior space, heavily curtained off and wary of intrusion, and opened only by invitation for viewing on occasions such as parties or teas. "<i>The essential, unknowability of each individual, and society's collaboration in the maintenance of a façade behind which lurked innumerable mysteries, were the themes which preoccupied many mid-century novelists.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Kate_Summerscale" title="Kate Summerscale">Kate Summerscale</a> quoting <i>historian Anthony S. Wohl</i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Journalism">Journalism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_journalism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of journalism in the United Kingdom">History of journalism in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_John_Everett_Millais_-_Peace_Concluded_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Sir_John_Everett_Millais_-_Peace_Concluded_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/200px-Sir_John_Everett_Millais_-_Peace_Concluded_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Sir_John_Everett_Millais_-_Peace_Concluded_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/300px-Sir_John_Everett_Millais_-_Peace_Concluded_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Sir_John_Everett_Millais_-_Peace_Concluded_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/400px-Sir_John_Everett_Millais_-_Peace_Concluded_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3616" data-file-height="4663" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_John_Everett_Millais_-_Peace_Concluded_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wounded British officer reading <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a>'s</i> report of the end of the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1817 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Barnes_(journalist)" title="Thomas Barnes (journalist)">Thomas Barnes</a> became general editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>; he was a political radical, a sharp critic of parliamentary hypocrisy and a champion of freedom of the press.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Under Barnes and his successor in 1841, <a href="/wiki/John_Thadeus_Delane" title="John Thadeus Delane">John Thadeus Delane</a>, the influence of <i>The Times</i> rose to great heights, especially in politics and in the financial district (the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a>). It spoke of reform.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Times</i> originated the practice of sending <a href="/wiki/War_correspondent" title="War correspondent">war correspondents</a> to cover particular conflicts. <a href="/wiki/W._H._Russell" class="mw-redirect" title="W. H. Russell">W. H. Russell</a> wrote immensely influential dispatches on the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> of 1853–1856; for the first time, the public could read about the reality of warfare. Russell wrote one dispatch that highlighted the surgeons' "inhumane barbarity" and the lack of ambulance care for wounded troops. Shocked and outraged, the public reacted in a backlash that led to major reforms especially in the provision of nursing, led by <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Guardian" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchester Guardian">Manchester Guardian</a></i> was founded in Manchester in 1821 by a group of <a href="/wiki/Non-conformist" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-conformist">non-conformist</a> businessmen. Its most famous editor, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Prestwich_Scott" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Prestwich Scott">Charles Prestwich Scott</a>, made the <i>Guardian</i> into a world-famous newspaper in the 1890s. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> in 1856 became the first penny newspaper in London. It was funded by advertising revenue based on a large audience.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:241px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomson,_The_crawlers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thomson%2C_The_crawlers.jpg/239px-Thomson%2C_The_crawlers.jpg" decoding="async" width="239" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thomson%2C_The_crawlers.jpg/359px-Thomson%2C_The_crawlers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Thomson%2C_The_crawlers.jpg/478px-Thomson%2C_The_crawlers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="689" data-file-height="926" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomson,_The_crawlers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>From 'Street Life in London', 1877, by John Thomson and Adolphe Smith.  ''“But old age, and want of proper food and rest, reduces them to a lethargic condition which can scarcely be preferable to death itself.''</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leisure">Leisure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_sands,_Ramsgate,_Kent,_England,_ca._1899.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/The_sands%2C_Ramsgate%2C_Kent%2C_England%2C_ca._1899.jpg/220px-The_sands%2C_Ramsgate%2C_Kent%2C_England%2C_ca._1899.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/The_sands%2C_Ramsgate%2C_Kent%2C_England%2C_ca._1899.jpg/330px-The_sands%2C_Ramsgate%2C_Kent%2C_England%2C_ca._1899.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/The_sands%2C_Ramsgate%2C_Kent%2C_England%2C_ca._1899.jpg/440px-The_sands%2C_Ramsgate%2C_Kent%2C_England%2C_ca._1899.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3540" data-file-height="2593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_sands,_Ramsgate,_Kent,_England,_ca._1899.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ramsgate" title="Ramsgate">Ramsgate</a> beach in 1899</div></div></div>
<p>Opportunities for leisure activities increased dramatically as real wages continued to grow and hours of work continued to decline. In urban areas the nine-hour workday became increasingly the norm; the Factory Act 1874 limited the working week to 56.5 hours, encouraging the movement towards an eventual eight-hour workday. Furthermore, a system of routine annual holidays came into play, starting with white-collar workers and moving into the working-class.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Some 200 seaside resorts emerged thanks to cheap hotels and inexpensive railway fares, widespread bank holidays and the fading of many religious prohibitions against secular activities on Sundays.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late Victorian era the leisure industry had emerged in all cities. It provided scheduled entertainment of suitable length at convenient locales at inexpensive prices. These included sporting events, music halls, and popular theatre. By 1880 football was no longer the preserve of the social elite, as it attracted large working-class audiences. Average attendance was 5000 in 1905, rising to 23,000 in 1913. That amounted to 6 million paying customers with a weekly turnover of £400,000. Sports by 1900 generated some three percent of the total gross national product. Professional sports were the norm, although some new activities reached an upscale amateur audience, such as lawn tennis and golf. Women were now allowed in some sports, such as archery, tennis, badminton and gymnastics.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing">Housing</span></h2>
<p>The very rapid growth in population in the 19th century in the cities included the new industrial and manufacturing cities, as well as service centres such as Edinburgh and London. The critical factor was financing, which was handled by building societies that dealt directly with large contracting firms.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Private renting from housing landlords was the dominant tenure. P. Kemp says this was usually of advantage to tenants.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> People moved in so rapidly that there was not enough capital to build adequate housing for everyone, so low income newcomers squeezed into increasingly overcrowded slums. Clean water, sanitation, and public health facilities were inadequate; the death rate was high, especially infant mortality, and tuberculosis among young adults. <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">Cholera</a> from polluted water and typhoid were endemic. Unlike rural areas, there were no famines such as the one which devastated Ireland in the 1840s.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty">Poverty</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Poverty_map_old_nichol_1889.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Poverty_map_old_nichol_1889.jpg/220px-Poverty_map_old_nichol_1889.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Poverty_map_old_nichol_1889.jpg/330px-Poverty_map_old_nichol_1889.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Poverty_map_old_nichol_1889.jpg/440px-Poverty_map_old_nichol_1889.jpg 2x" data-file-width="556" data-file-height="417" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Poverty_map_old_nichol_1889.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Booth_(philanthropist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Booth (philanthropist)">Charles Booth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Poverty_map" title="Poverty map">poverty map</a> showing the <a href="/wiki/Old_Nichol" title="Old Nichol">Old Nichol</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Slum" title="Slum">slum</a> in the <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_London" title="East End of London">East End of London</a>. Published 1889 in <a href="/wiki/Life_and_Labour_of_the_People_in_London" title="Life and Labour of the People in London">Life and Labour of the People in London</a>. The red areas are "middle class, well-to-do", light blue areas are "poor, 18s to 21s a week for a moderate family", dark blue areas are "very poor, casual, chronic want", and black areas are the "lowest class...occasional labourers, street sellers, loafers, criminals and semi-criminals".</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victorian_Bishopgate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Victorian_Bishopgate.jpg/220px-Victorian_Bishopgate.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Victorian_Bishopgate.jpg/330px-Victorian_Bishopgate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Victorian_Bishopgate.jpg/440px-Victorian_Bishopgate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="313" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victorian_Bishopgate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Working class life in Victorian <a href="/wiki/Wetherby" title="Wetherby">Wetherby</a>, West Yorkshire</div></div></div>
<p>19th-century Britain saw a huge population increase accompanied by rapid urbanisation stimulated by the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Wage rates improved steadily; real wages (after taking inflation into account) were 65 percent higher in 1901, compared to 1871. Much of the money was saved, as the number of depositors in savings banks rose from 430,000 in 1831, to 5.2&#160;million in 1887, and their deposits from £14&#160;million to over £90&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> People flooded into industrial areas and commercial cities faster than housing could be built, resulting in overcrowding and lagging sanitation facilities such as fresh water and sewage.
</p><p>These problems were magnified in London, where the population grew at record rates. Large houses were turned into flats and tenements, and as landlords failed to maintain these dwellings, <a href="/wiki/Slum" title="Slum">slum</a> housing developed. <a href="/wiki/Kellow_Chesney" title="Kellow Chesney">Kellow Chesney</a> described the situation as follows: "Hideous slums, some of them acres wide, some no more than crannies of obscure misery, make up a substantial part of the metropolis... In big, once handsome houses, thirty or more people of all ages may inhabit a single room."<sup id="cite_ref-dan_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dan-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Significant changes happened in the British <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Poor_Law_system#1800s" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of the Poor Law system">Poor Law</a> system in <a href="/wiki/English_Poor_Laws" title="English Poor Laws">England and Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Poor_Laws" title="Scottish Poor Laws">Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Irish_Poor_Laws" title="Irish Poor Laws">Ireland</a>. These included a large expansion in <a href="/wiki/Workhouses" class="mw-redirect" title="Workhouses">workhouses</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Poorhouses" class="mw-redirect" title="Poorhouses">poorhouses</a> in Scotland), although with changing populations during the era.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_labour">Child labour</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coaltub.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Coaltub.png/220px-Coaltub.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/Coaltub.png 1.5x" data-file-width="282" data-file-height="171" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coaltub.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Girl pulling a coal tub in mine. From official report of the parliamentary commission in the mid-19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-ashley_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashley-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The early Victorian era before the reforms of the 1840s became notorious for <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">the employment of young children</a> in factories and mines and as <a href="/wiki/Chimney_sweep" title="Chimney sweep">chimney sweeps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Child labour played an important role in the Industrial Revolution from its outset: novelist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, for example, worked at the age of 12 in a <a href="/wiki/Blacking_(polish)" class="mw-redirect" title="Blacking (polish)">blacking</a> factory, with his family in a <a href="/wiki/Debtors%27_prison" title="Debtors&#39; prison">debtors' prison</a>.  Reformers wanted the children in school: in 1840 only about 20 percent of the children in London had any schooling. By 1860 about half of the children between 5 and 15 were in school (including <a href="/wiki/Sunday_school" title="Sunday school">Sunday school</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-cody_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cody-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The children of the poor were expected to help towards the family budget, often working long hours in dangerous jobs for low wages.<sup id="cite_ref-dan_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dan-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Agile boys were employed by the chimney sweeps; small children were employed to scramble under machinery to retrieve cotton bobbins; and children were also employed to work in <a href="/wiki/History_of_coal_mining" title="History of coal mining">coal mines</a>, crawling through tunnels too narrow and low for adults. Children also worked as errand boys, <a href="/wiki/Crossing_sweeper" title="Crossing sweeper">crossing sweepers</a>, shoe blacks, or sold matches, flowers, and other cheap goods.<sup id="cite_ref-dan_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dan-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Some children undertook work as apprentices to respectable trades, such as building, or as <a href="/wiki/Domestic_worker" title="Domestic worker">domestic servants</a> (there were over 120,000 domestic servants in London in the mid 19th century). Working hours were long: builders might work 64 hours a week in summer and 52 in winter, while domestic servants were theoretically on duty 80-hours a week.
</p>
<blockquote><p>Mother bides at home, she is troubled with bad breath, and is sair weak in her body from early labour. I am wrought with sister and brother, it is very sore work; cannot say how many rakes or journeys I make from pit's bottom to wall face and back, thinks about 30 or 25 on the average; the distance varies from 100 to 250 fathom. I carry about 1 cwt. and a quarter on my back; have to stoop much and creep through water, which is frequently up to the calves of my legs.
</p><dl><dd>— Isabella Read, 12 years old, coal-bearer, testimony gathered by Ashley's Mines Commission 1842<sup id="cite_ref-ashley_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashley-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl></blockquote>
<p>As early as 1802 and 1819, <a href="/wiki/Factory_Acts" title="Factory Acts">Factory Acts</a> were passed to limit the working hours of children in factories and <a href="/wiki/Cotton_mill" title="Cotton mill">cotton mills</a> to 12 hours per day. These acts were largely ineffective and after radical agitation, by for example the "Short Time Committees" in 1831, a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Commission">Royal Commission</a> recommended in 1833 that children aged 11–18 should work a maximum of 12 hours per day, children aged 9–11 a maximum of eight hours, and children under the age of nine should no longer be permitted to work. This act, however, only applied to the <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a>, and further agitation led to another act in 1847 limiting both adults and children to 10-hour working days.<sup id="cite_ref-cody_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cody-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morality">Morality</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Victorian_morality" title="Victorian morality">Victorian morality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Victorian_era" title="Women in the Victorian era">Women in the Victorian era</a></div>
<p>Victorian morality was a surprising new reality. The changes in moral standards and actual behaviour across the British were profound. Historian <a href="/wiki/Harold_Perkin" title="Harold Perkin">Harold Perkin</a> wrote:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Between 1780 and 1850 the English ceased to be one of the most aggressive, brutal, rowdy, outspoken, riotous, cruel and bloodthirsty nations in the world and became one of the most inhibited, polite, orderly, tender-minded, prudish and hypocritical.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Historians continue to debate the various causes of this dramatic change. <a href="/wiki/Asa_Briggs" title="Asa Briggs">Asa Briggs</a> emphasizes the strong reaction against the French Revolution, and the need to focus British efforts on its defeat and not be diverged by pleasurable sins. Briggs also stresses the powerful role of the evangelical movement among the Nonconformists, as well as the Evangelical faction inside the established Church of England.  The religious and political reformers set up organizations that monitored behaviour, and pushed for government action.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the higher social classes, there was a marked decline in gambling, horse races, and obscene theatres; there was much less heavy gambling or patronage of upscale houses of prostitution. The highly visible debauchery characteristic of aristocratic England in the early 19th century simply disappeared.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historians agree that the middle classes not only professed high personal moral standards, but actually followed them. There is a debate whether the working classes followed suit. Moralists in the late 19th century such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Mayhew" title="Henry Mayhew">Henry Mayhew</a> decried the slums for their supposed high levels of cohabitation without marriage and illegitimate births. However new research using computerized matching of data files shows that the rates of cohabitation were quite low—under 5%—for the working class and the poor. By contrast, in 21st-century Britain nearly half of all children are born outside marriage, and nine in ten newlyweds have been cohabitating.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Crime.2C_police_and_prisons"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crime,_police_and_prisons">Crime, police and prisons</span></h3>
<p>Crime was getting exponentially worse. There were 4,065 arrests for criminal offenses in 1805, tripling to 14,437 in 1835 and doubling to 31,309 in 1842 in England and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>18th-century British criminology had emphasized severe punishment. Slowly capital punishment was replaced by transportation, first to the American colonies and then to Australia,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> and, especially, by long-term incarceration in newly built prisons. As one historian points out, "Public and violent punishment which attacked the body by branding, whipping, and hanging was giving way to reformation of the mind of the criminal by breaking his spirit, and encouraging him to reflect on his shame, before labour and religion transformed his character."<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Crime rates went up, leading to calls for harsher measures ito stop the 'flood of criminals' released under the penal servitude system. The reaction from the committee set up under the commissioner of prisons, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Frederick_du_Cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund Frederick du Cane">Edmund Frederick du Cane</a>, was to increase minimum sentences for many offences with deterrent principles of 'hard labour, hard fare, and a hard bed'.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> As the prisons grew more numerous, they became more depraved. Historian S. G. Checkland says, "It was sunk in promiscuity and squalor, jailers' tyranny and greed, and administrative confusion."<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> In 1877 du Cane encouraged <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Disraeli</a>'s government to remove all prisons from local government; he held a firm grip on the prison system till his forced retirement in 1895. By the 1890s, the prison population was over 20,000.
</p><p>By the Victorian era, <a href="/wiki/Penal_transportation" title="Penal transportation">penal transportation</a> to Australia was falling out of use since it did not reduce crime rates.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The British penal system underwent a transition from harsh punishment to reform, education, and training for post-prison livelihoods. The reforms were controversial and contested. In 1877-1914 era a series of major legislative reforms enabled significant improvement in the penal system. In 1877, the previously localized prisons were nationalized in the Home Office under a Prison Commission. The Prison Act of 1898 enabled the Home Secretary to impose multiple reforms on his own initiative, without going through the politicized process of Parliament. The Probation of Offenders Act of 1907 introduced a new probation system that drastically cut down the prison population, while providing a mechanism for transition back to normal life. The Criminal Justice Administration Act of 1914 required courts to allow a reasonable time before imprisonment was ordered for people who did not pay their fines. Previously tens of thousands of prisoners had been sentenced solely for that reason. The Borstal system after 1908 was organized to reclaim young offenders, and the Children Act of 1908 prohibited imprisonment under age 14, and strictly limited that of ages 14 to 16. The principal reformer was Sir <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Ruggles-Brise" title="Evelyn Ruggles-Brise">Evelyn Ruggles-Brise</a>, the chair of the Prison Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prostitution">Prostitution</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Victorian_morality#Prostitution" title="Victorian morality">Victorian morality §&#160;Prostitution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_the_United_Kingdom#19th_century" title="Prostitution in the United Kingdom">Prostitution in the United Kingdom §&#160;19th century</a></div>
<p>Prostitution had been a factor in city life for centuries. The reformers started mobilizing in the late 1840s; major news organisations, clergymen and single women became increasingly concerned about prostitution, which came to be known as "The Great Social Evil".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Estimates of the number of prostitutes in London in the 1850s vary widely, but in his landmark study, <i>Prostitution</i>, <a href="/wiki/William_Acton_(doctor)" title="William Acton (doctor)">William Acton</a> reported that the police estimated there were 8,600 in London alone in 1857.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Events">Events</span></h2>
<dl><dt>1832</dt>
<dd>Passage of the first <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act" title="Reform Act">Reform Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-greenhavenpress_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenhavenpress-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Launch-of-the-SS-GB.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Launch-of-the-SS-GB.jpg/220px-Launch-of-the-SS-GB.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Launch-of-the-SS-GB.jpg/330px-Launch-of-the-SS-GB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Launch-of-the-SS-GB.jpg/440px-Launch-of-the-SS-GB.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1507" data-file-height="913" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Launch-of-the-SS-GB.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1843 launch of the <i><a href="/wiki/SS_Great_Britain" title="SS Great Britain">Great Britain</a></i>, the revolutionary ship of <a href="/wiki/Isambard_Kingdom_Brunel" title="Isambard Kingdom Brunel">Isambard Kingdom Brunel</a></div></div></div></dd>
<dt>1837</dt>
<dd>Ascension of Queen Victoria to the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-greenhavenpress_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenhavenpress-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt>1838</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Balta_Liman" title="Treaty of Balta Liman">Treaty of Balta Liman</a> (Great Britain trade alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>).</dd>
<dt>1839</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a> (1839–42) fought between Britain and China.</dd>
<dt>1840</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> marries <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg-Saalfield</a>. He had been <a href="/wiki/Naturalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Naturalisation">naturalised</a> and granted the British style of <i><a href="/wiki/Royal_Highness" title="Royal Highness">Royal Highness</a></i> beforehand. For the next 17 years, he was known as <i>HRH</i> Prince Albert.</dd>
<dt>1840</dt>
<dd>New Zealand becomes a British colony, through the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Waitangi" title="Treaty of Waitangi">Treaty of Waitangi</a>. No longer part of New South Wales<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/220px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/330px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/440px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1383" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a>: British ships approaching <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Canton</a> in May 1841</div></div></div></dd>
<dt>1842</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Treaty of Nanking</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_Elphinstone%27s_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Massacre of Elphinstone&#39;s Army">Massacre of Elphinstone's Army</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Demography of Afghanistan">Afghans</a> in Afghanistan results in the death or incarceration of 16,500 soldiers and civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mines_Act_of_1842" class="mw-redirect" title="Mines Act of 1842">Mines Act of 1842</a> banned women/children from working in coal, <a href="/wiki/Iron_mine" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron mine">iron</a>, lead and <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> mining.<sup id="cite_ref-greenhavenpress_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenhavenpress-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_London_News" title="The Illustrated London News">The Illustrated London News</a></i> was first published.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt>1845</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Irish famine</a> begins. Within 5 years it would become the UK's <a href="/wiki/List_of_disasters_of_the_United_Kingdom_and_preceding_states" class="mw-redirect" title="List of disasters of the United Kingdom and preceding states">worst human disaster</a>, with starvation and emigration reducing the population of Ireland itself by over 50%. The famine permanently changed Ireland's and Scotland's demographics and became a rallying point for nationalist sentiment that pervaded British politics for much of the following century.</dd>
<dt>1846</dt>
<dd>Repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-greenhavenpress_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenhavenpress-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt>1848</dt>
<dd>Death of around 2,000 people a week in a <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> epidemic.</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Pollard_-_The_Last_of_the_Mail_Coaches_at_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/James_Pollard_-_The_Last_of_the_Mail_Coaches_at_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-James_Pollard_-_The_Last_of_the_Mail_Coaches_at_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/James_Pollard_-_The_Last_of_the_Mail_Coaches_at_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-James_Pollard_-_The_Last_of_the_Mail_Coaches_at_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/James_Pollard_-_The_Last_of_the_Mail_Coaches_at_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-James_Pollard_-_The_Last_of_the_Mail_Coaches_at_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5101" data-file-height="3308" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Pollard_-_The_Last_of_the_Mail_Coaches_at_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The last of the mail coaches at <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>, 1848</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>1850</dt>
<dd>Restoration of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in the United Kingdom">Roman Catholic</a> hierarchy in England and Wales. (Scotland did not <a href="/wiki/Restoration_of_the_Scottish_hierarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Restoration of the Scottish hierarchy">follow until 1878</a>.)</dd>
<dt>1851</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Exhibition" class="mw-redirect" title="The Great Exhibition">The Great Exhibition</a> (the first World's Fair) is held at the Crystal Palace,<sup id="cite_ref-greenhavenpress_135-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenhavenpress-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> with great success and international attention. The <a href="/wiki/Victorian_gold_rush" title="Victorian gold rush">Victorian gold rush</a>. In ten years the Australian population nearly tripled.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal_Palace_-_interior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Crystal_Palace_-_interior.jpg/220px-Crystal_Palace_-_interior.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Crystal_Palace_-_interior.jpg/330px-Crystal_Palace_-_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Crystal_Palace_-_interior.jpg/440px-Crystal_Palace_-_interior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1761" data-file-height="1107" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal_Palace_-_interior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Exhibition" class="mw-redirect" title="The Great Exhibition">The Great Exhibition</a> in London. The United Kingdom was the first country in the world to industrialise.</div></div></div></dd>
<dt>1854</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>: Britain, France and Turkey declare limited war on Russia. Russia loses.</dd>
<dt>1857</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Mutiny</a>, a concentrated revolt in northern India against the rule of <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">the privately owned British East India Company</a>. Violent findings and massacre and sin British victory. The East India Company is replaced by the British government beginning the period of the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>.</dd>
<dt>1858</dt>
<dd>The Prime Minister, Lord Palmerston, responds to the <a href="/wiki/Orsini_affair" title="Orsini affair">Orsini plot</a> against French Emperor <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon III of France">Napoleon III</a>, the bombs for which were purchased in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, by attempting to make such acts a <a href="/wiki/Felony" title="Felony">felony</a>; the resulting uproar forces him to resign.</dd>
<dt>1859</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> publishes <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i>, which leads to <a href="/wiki/Reaction_to_Darwin%27s_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Reaction to Darwin&#39;s theory">various reactions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-greenhavenpress_135-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenhavenpress-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Victoria and Albert's first grandchild, Prince Wilhelm of Prussia, is born&#160;– he later became <a href="/wiki/William_II,_German_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="William II, German Emperor">William II, German Emperor</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> publishes <i><a href="/wiki/On_Liberty" title="On Liberty">On Liberty</a></i>, a defence of the famous <a href="/wiki/Harm_principle" title="Harm principle">harm principle</a>.</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Return_visit_of_the_Viceroy_to_the_Maharaja_of_Cashmere.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Return_visit_of_the_Viceroy_to_the_Maharaja_of_Cashmere.jpg/220px-Return_visit_of_the_Viceroy_to_the_Maharaja_of_Cashmere.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Return_visit_of_the_Viceroy_to_the_Maharaja_of_Cashmere.jpg/330px-Return_visit_of_the_Viceroy_to_the_Maharaja_of_Cashmere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Return_visit_of_the_Viceroy_to_the_Maharaja_of_Cashmere.jpg/440px-Return_visit_of_the_Viceroy_to_the_Maharaja_of_Cashmere.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3997" data-file-height="2799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Return_visit_of_the_Viceroy_to_the_Maharaja_of_Cashmere.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Governor-General of India <a href="/wiki/Lord_Canning" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Canning">Lord Canning</a> meets Maharaja <a href="/wiki/Ranbir_Singh_(Maharaja)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ranbir Singh (Maharaja)">Ranbir Singh</a> of Jammu and Kashmir, 1860</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>1861</dt>
<dd>Death of <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">Prince Albert</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-greenhavenpress_135-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenhavenpress-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Queen Victoria refuses to go out in public for many years, and when she did she wore a widow's <a href="/wiki/Bonnet_(headgear)" title="Bonnet (headgear)">bonnet</a> instead of the crown.</dd>
<dt>1865</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Adventures_in_Wonderland" title="Alice&#39;s Adventures in Wonderland">Alice's Adventures in Wonderland</a></i> is published.</dd>
<dt>1866</dt>
<dd>An angry crowd in London, protesting against <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">John Russell</a>'s resignation as Prime Minister, is barred from <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Law enforcement in the United Kingdom">police</a>; they tear down iron <a href="/wiki/Guard_rail" title="Guard rail">railings</a> and trample on <a href="/wiki/Garden" title="Garden">flower beds</a>. Disturbances like this convince Derby and Disraeli of the need for further parliamentary reform.</dd>
<dt>1867</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Act,_1867" title="Constitution Act, 1867">Constitution Act, 1867</a> passes and <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a> becomes Dominion of Canada.<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:D%C3%A9fense_de_Rorke%27s_Drift.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/D%C3%A9fense_de_Rorke%27s_Drift.jpg/220px-D%C3%A9fense_de_Rorke%27s_Drift.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/D%C3%A9fense_de_Rorke%27s_Drift.jpg/330px-D%C3%A9fense_de_Rorke%27s_Drift.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/D%C3%A9fense_de_Rorke%27s_Drift.jpg/440px-D%C3%A9fense_de_Rorke%27s_Drift.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2148" data-file-height="1407" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:D%C3%A9fense_de_Rorke%27s_Drift.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The defence of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Rorke%27s_Drift" title="Battle of Rorke&#39;s Drift">Rorke's Drift</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zulu_War" title="Anglo-Zulu War">Anglo-Zulu War</a> of 1879</div></div></div></dd>
<dt>1875</dt>
<dd>Britain purchased Egypt's shares in the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a><sup id="cite_ref-greenhavenpress_135-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenhavenpress-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> as the African nation was forced to raise money to pay off its <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_Pasha#Suez_Canal" title="Isma&#39;il Pasha">debts</a>.</dd>
<dt>1876</dt>
<dd>Scottish-born inventor <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a> patents the telephone.</dd>
<dt>1878</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berlin_(1878)" title="Treaty of Berlin (1878)">Treaty of Berlin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> becomes a <a href="/wiki/Crown_colony" title="Crown colony">Crown colony</a>.</dd>
<dt>1879</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Isandlwana" title="Battle of Isandlwana">Battle of Isandlwana</a> is the first major encounter in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zulu_War" title="Anglo-Zulu War">Anglo-Zulu War</a>.</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prempeh-124-palaver-and-submission.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Prempeh-124-palaver-and-submission.jpg/220px-Prempeh-124-palaver-and-submission.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Prempeh-124-palaver-and-submission.jpg/330px-Prempeh-124-palaver-and-submission.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Prempeh-124-palaver-and-submission.jpg/440px-Prempeh-124-palaver-and-submission.jpg 2x" data-file-width="618" data-file-height="377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prempeh-124-palaver-and-submission.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Following the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Anglo-Ashanti_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Fourth Anglo-Ashanti War">Fourth Anglo-Ashanti War</a> in 1896, the British proclaimed a protectorate over the <a href="/wiki/Ashanti_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Ashanti Kingdom">Ashanti Kingdom</a>.</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>1881</dt>
<dd>The British suffer defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Majuba_Hill" title="Battle of Majuba Hill">Battle of Majuba Hill</a>, leading to the signing of a peace treaty and later the <a href="/wiki/Pretoria_Convention" title="Pretoria Convention">Pretoria Convention</a>, between the British and the reinstated <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">South African Republic</a>, ending the <a href="/wiki/First_Boer_War" title="First Boer War">First Boer War</a>. Sometimes claimed to mark the beginning of the decline of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Little_Wars_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Little_Wars-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt>1882</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British troops</a> begin the <a href="/wiki/British_occupation_of_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="British occupation of Egypt">occupation of Egypt</a> by taking the Suez Canal, to secure the vital <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade route</a> and passage to India, and the country becomes a <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a>.</dd>
<dt>1884</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a> is founded in London by a group of middle-class intellectuals, including <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Society of Friends">Quaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_R._Pease" title="Edward R. Pease">Edward R. Pease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Havelock_Ellis" title="Havelock Ellis">Havelock Ellis</a> and <a href="/wiki/E._Nesbit" title="E. Nesbit">E. Nesbit</a>, to promote socialism.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Prince_Leopold,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Prince Leopold, Duke of Albany">Prince Leopold, Duke of Albany</a> dies.</dd>
<dt>1885</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Blackpool_Electric_Tramway_Company" title="Blackpool Electric Tramway Company">Blackpool Electric Tramway Company</a> starts the first electric tram service in the United Kingdom.</dd>
<dt>1886</dt>
<dd>Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> and the Liberal Party tries passing the <a href="/wiki/First_Irish_Home_Rule_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="First Irish Home Rule Bill">First Irish Home Rule Bill</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> rejects it.<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Daimler_wagonette_National_Library_of_Ireland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Daimler_wagonette_National_Library_of_Ireland.jpg/220px-Daimler_wagonette_National_Library_of_Ireland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Daimler_wagonette_National_Library_of_Ireland.jpg/330px-Daimler_wagonette_National_Library_of_Ireland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Daimler_wagonette_National_Library_of_Ireland.jpg/440px-Daimler_wagonette_National_Library_of_Ireland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2503" data-file-height="1890" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Daimler_wagonette_National_Library_of_Ireland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Daimler_Company" title="Daimler Company">Daimler</a> Wagonette, Ireland, c. 1899</div></div></div></dd>
<dt>1888</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Serial_killer" title="Serial killer">serial killer</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Jack_the_Ripper" title="Jack the Ripper">Jack the Ripper</a> murders and mutilates five (and possibly more) prostitutes on the streets of London.</dd>
<dt>1889</dt>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Emily_Williamson" title="Emily Williamson">Emily Williamson</a> founds the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a>.<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boer_War_officers_P03206.001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Boer_War_officers_P03206.001.jpg/220px-Boer_War_officers_P03206.001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Boer_War_officers_P03206.001.jpg/330px-Boer_War_officers_P03206.001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/99/Boer_War_officers_P03206.001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="425" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boer_War_officers_P03206.001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British and Australian officers in South Africa during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a></div></div></div></dd>
<dt>1870–1891</dt>
<dd>Under the <a href="/wiki/Elementary_Education_Act_1870" title="Elementary Education Act 1870">Elementary Education Act 1870</a>, basic <a href="/wiki/State_school" title="State school">State Education</a> becomes free for every child under the age of 10.<sup id="cite_ref-1870_Edu_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1870_Edu-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg/220px-MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg/330px-MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="266" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Workmen leaving <a href="/wiki/Platt_Brothers" title="Platt Brothers">Platt's Works</a>, Oldham, 1900</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>1898</dt>
<dd>British and Egyptian troops led by <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener_of_Khartoum" class="mw-redirect" title="Horatio Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener of Khartoum">Horatio Kitchener</a> defeat the <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdist</a> forces at the battle of Omdurman, thus establishing British dominance in the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>. Winston Churchill takes part in the British cavalry charge at Omdurman.</dd>
<dt>1899</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> is fought between the British Empire and the two independent <a href="/wiki/Boer_Republics" title="Boer Republics">Boer</a> republics. The <a href="/wiki/Boer" title="Boer">Boers</a> finally surrendered and the British annexed the <a href="/wiki/Boer_republics" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer republics">Boer republics</a>.</dd>
<dt>1901</dt>
<dd>The death of Victoria sees the end of this era. The ascension of her eldest son, Edward, begins the <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian era</a>.</dd></dl>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span></h2>
<ul><li>Adams, James Eli, ed. <i>Encyclopedia of the Victorian Era</i> (4 Vol. 2004), short essays on a wide range of topics by experts</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Richard_Altick" title="Richard Altick">Altick, Richard Daniel</a>. <i>Victorian People and Ideas: A Companion for the Modern Reader of Victorian Literature</i>. (1974)  <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/victorianpeoplei00alti">online free</a></li>
<li>Bailey, Peter. <i>Leisure and class in Victorian England: Rational recreation and the contest for control, 1830–1885</i> (Routledge, 2014).</li>
<li>Bourne, Kenneth. <i>The foreign policy of Victorian England, 1830–1902</i> (Oxford UP, 1970), contains a short narrative history and 147 "Selected documents" on pp 195–504.</li>
<li>Boyd, Kelly and Rohan McWilliam, eds. <i>The Victorian Studies Reader</i> (2007) 467pp; articles and excerpts by scholars <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amazon.com/dp/0415355796">excepts and text search</a></li>
<li>Bright, J. Franck. <i>A History of England. Period 4: Growth of Democracy: Victoria 1837–1880</i> (1902)<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=9CJAAQAAMAAJ">online</a> 608pp; highly detailed older political narrative
<ul><li><i>A History of England: Period V. Imperial Reaction, Victoria, 1880‒1901</i> (1904) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historyofengland05brig/page/n5">online</a></li></ul></li>

Palgrave Macmillan: 2001. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-312-29335-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-312-29335-6">0-312-29335-6</a>.</li>

Norton &amp; Company: 2004. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-393-05209-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-393-05209-5">0-393-05209-5</a>.</li>

<li>Heffer, Simon. <i>The Age of Decadence: Britain 1880 to 1914</i> (2017), wide-ranging scholarly survey.</li>
